        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";

            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="0a984dcd789546dbb951e7f2b9ba9b2c"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="0a984dcd789546dbb951e7f2b9ba9b2c">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '4a01f1eb-ecea-40b2-b1d2-4cdef7852e43'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = 'FCEFFB2734D262F2B22BC97223AA5FBC';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d42358064" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Recycling waste biomass to pyrolytic carbon has become a development direction of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs)&nbsp;anodes. However, it remains a challenge to precisely control the composition and structure of biomass to modify the properties of derived carbon. Herein, a strategy of hydrolyzing cellulose in phellem with sulfuric acid is proposed, which can promote cellulose fracture, reduce the graphitization and increase the content of closed pores in hard carbon. Accordingly, after the regulation of closed pore structure, the reversible capacity increased from 207 to 330 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, realizing an increase of ≈130 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in the plateau region and the initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) is enhanced from 78% to 90%. When applied in full cell with O3-Na[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, it showed an energy density of 247&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. This strategy has certain universality, and it provides the feasibility to use low-value cellulose-containing biomass to fabricate high-performance hard carbon materials.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202407341-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202407341-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>SIBs have the characteristics of low cost, wide operating temperature and high safety, which are expected to become an promising alternative beyond lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0001_R_d42357991e462" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Energy Environ. Sci.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among various types of anodes for SIBs, hard carbon is considered to be a potential material as its abundant raw materials and high economic benefit.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0002_R_d42357991e467" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the microstructure of hard carbon is complex and difficult to control, and there are also limitations such as low ICE, poor rate performance, and unclear Na storage mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0003_R_d42357991e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Sci.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>From the perspective of microstructure, hard carbon is a kind of non-graphitized carbons with complex structure formed by stacking, extending, and winding short-range ordered pseudo-graphite crystallites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0004_R_d42357991e480" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Mater. Today">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The random, disordered structure provides a variety of Na storage sites in hard carbons, including adsorption on edges, intercalation between layers, and filling in micropores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0004_R_d42357991e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Mater. Today">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Different Na storage processes have different characteristics, for example, the adsorption on edges, defects, or heteroatom sites usually has faster kinetics, which is conductive to the improvement of the rate performance of hard carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0005_R_d42357991e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nano Lett.">5</a>-<a href="#smll202407341-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0007_R_d42357991e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, this adsorption behavior often produces a large irreversible capacity during the first cycle, resulting in a low ICE, which is harmful to the energy density of full batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0008_R_d42357991e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nano Energy">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In recent years, researchers paid more attention to the improvement of plateau capacity related to closed pores because of the pursuance of high energy density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0009_R_d42357991e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Energy Lett.">9</a>-<a href="#smll202407341-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0011_R_d42357991e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Since the filling process of Na<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;in the closed pores is often highly reversible and the reaction potential is low, it is a feasible solution to simultaneously improve the ICE and reduce the working potential of hard carbon, which is very conductive to the improvement of the energy density of the full cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0009_R_d42357991e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Energy Lett.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The usage of templates and pore formers increases the cost and complexity of the preparation for hard carbon, although some works were reported to promote the formation of closed pores successfully. In the reported studies, templates or pore formers were usually introduced during the carbonization process to promote the formation of closed pores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0009_R_d42357991e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Energy Lett.">9</a>, <a href="#smll202407341-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0010_R_d42357991e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Biomass precursors have their unique structure which can inherit to their derived carbons after carbonization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0012_R_d42357991e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Storage Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, directly obtaining closed-pore structure from biomass material precursors has broad research prospects.</p>
         
         <p>The modification of waste biomass into high-performance anode materials of SIBs can meet the demand for high energy density and the conception of green development at the same time. Hu et al. reported a waste cork derived hard carbon that can effectively store Na<sup>+</sup> by adjusting the opening and closing of pores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0011_R_d42357991e529" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By increasing the pyrolysis temperature to reduce the open pore (related to ICE) and increase the closed pore (related to plateau capacity), it has a Na storage capacity of 360 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in half cell and an energy density of 230&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> in full cell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0011_R_d42357991e539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although some progress has been made in using biomass-derived hard carbon as anodes for SIBs, biomass-derived carbons still face some bottlenecks. Due to the complex composition and various structures of biomass precursors, the structures and properties of carbon materials produced by direct pyrolysis vary. As far as wood-based biomass is concerned, the relationship between the structure and properties of pyrolytic carbon and the composition and structure of precursors, as well as the structural evolution during pyrolysis, have not yet been clarified, resulting in no clear guide for choosing and modifying wood-based precursors. In our previous work, we proposed the effect of cellulose crystallinity in wood-based biomass on the closed pore structure of pyrolytic carbon and developed a method to modify the cellulose structure to improve the performance of derived hard carbon.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0013_R_d42357991e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - SusMat">13</a>, <a href="#smll202407341-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0014_R_d42357991e544" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Commun.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, for some softwoods, the performance of pyrolytic carbon is often poor, and it is difficult to use them directly. In addition, during the high-temperature carbonization process, the thicker fiber structure in wood tends to be pyrolyzed to form excessive graphitized structure, which is not conducive to the storage of Na<sup>+</sup> (because of the thermodynamic instability of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalated between graphite layers).</p>
         
         <p>In this work, using waste wood-based biomass as sources, a carbonization model was established, and the closed pore structure of waste biomass-derived carbon was mainly regulated. A strategy for hydrolyzing cellulose in phellem with sulfuric acid was designed to control the structure of precursors and pyrolytic carbon. Through analysis and characterization, it is found that the introduction of sulfuric acid hydrolysis promotes the fracture of cellulose, which reduces the graphitization degree of its derived carbon and increases the closed pores content of pyrolytic carbon. The ICE of optimized hard carbon is 90%, and the reversible capacity is increased from 207 to 330 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, especially in the plateau part. The full battery assembled with Na[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode shows a high energy density of 247&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. Surprisingly, this proposed method has certain universality, and provides the feasibility for efficient recycling of low-value cellulose-containing biomass to fabricate high-performance hard carbon anode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202407341-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202407341-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The stoppers for thermos bottles bought from market are mainly made from phellem, which have large economic benefits if they can be recycled to carbonaceous anodes for SIBs. Because of the difference of the composition and structure of biomass precursors, hard carbons directly made from biomass generally demonstrate much different properties and performances, although there have been many attempts at biomass-derived hard carbon in the field of energy storage and management. That means, the structure and function of derived hard carbon highly depend on the structure of cellulose in precursor.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0015_R_d42357991e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Inspired by the hydrolysis of cellulose, the acid treatment was introduced for engineering the structure of cellulose in phellem and thus improving the Na storage performance of low-cost phellem-derived hard carbon (<b>Scheme</b> <a href="#smll202407341-fig-0006">1</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202407341-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/37816844-6db2-4a03-92b1-c7db7b907ae1/smll202407341-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/37816844-6db2-4a03-92b1-c7db7b907ae1/smll202407341-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/85af5340-70e0-47f2-b503-ae86e0b149f1/smll202407341-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/37816844-6db2-4a03-92b1-c7db7b907ae1/smll202407341-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202407341-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202407341" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Illustration of typical process of hydrolyzing cellulose chain with sulfuric acid to promote closed-pores formation.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The composition and structural evolution of cellulose after sulfuric acid treatment were studied by X-ray diffraction (XRD) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202407341-fig-0001">1a</a>). The untreated phellem (Phe) shows two characteristic peaks at 15° and 22° corresponding to crystalline cellulose.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0016_R_d42357991e618" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Text. Res. J.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The phellem treated with sulfuric acid for 1&nbsp;h (H1-Phe) exhibits a wide peak near 20°, indicating the disordered structure. By sulfuric acid hydrolysis, the crystallized cellulose decomposition is promoted, forming disorder and non-crystalline structure. Our previous work has confirmed the correction between the crystalline degree of cellulose and the structure of derived carbon. Thus, in this work, we utilize the decrystallization of cellulose in phellem to inhibit the excessive formation of graphitized carbon domains, engineering defects and closed pores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0014_R_d42357991e623" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Commun.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further, the morphology and microstructure of the as-prepared samples were characterized using high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) and scanning electron microscopy (SEM) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0001">1b,c</a>; Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Compared with long chain structure of the fibers in Phe (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0001">1b</a>), the long chain structure of the fibers cannot be obviously observed in the HRTEM image of H1-Phe (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0001">1c</a>), which should be transformed into short-range disordered structure instead. Note that the hydrolysis reaction mainly involves the change of segmental length, while the main components in wood do not change significantly, which will be further analyzed below.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0017_R_d42357991e645" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Eng. J.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202407341-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7fad785b-ea40-49e1-8cfb-bb115cda0dcf/smll202407341-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7fad785b-ea40-49e1-8cfb-bb115cda0dcf/smll202407341-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a8d2fbb5-fb55-456f-aac7-02ae2d8e920d/smll202407341-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7fad785b-ea40-49e1-8cfb-bb115cda0dcf/smll202407341-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202407341-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202407341" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The characterization of precursors with and without treatment. a) XRD patterns; b) HRTEM image of Phe; c) HRTEM image of H1-Phe; d) FTIR spectra; e)Weight loss curves; f) Elemental analysis from XPS.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0001">1d</a> shows Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) curves of the phellem samples before and after sulfuric acid treatment. As seen, the absorption bands in the region ≈1600, 1420–1510, and 1000 cm<sup>−1</sup> are corresponding to the stretching vibration of C═O in hemicellulose, the aromatic stretching vibration in the lignin molecule, and the antisymmetric C─O─C bridge stretching in cellulose, respectively, which are the main components in phellem.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0018_R_d42357991e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Vib. Spectrosc.">18</a>, <a href="#smll202407341-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0019_R_d42357991e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Holzforschung">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Comparing the two curves, the abovementioned characteristic bands do not vary significantly after sulfuric acid treatment, indicating that the acid treatment did not change the composition significantly. Moreover, the band strength of C─O─H (1200 cm<sup>−1</sup>) corresponding to cellulose terminal group is obviously stronger in acid-treated sample than that in untreated sample, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0001">1d</a>, indicating that the long chain of cellulose was broken and more terminal group were exposed. In addition, the composition and pyrolysis characteristics of Phe and H1-Phe can be studied by thermogravimetric (TG) analysis. According to existing literature, the thermal stability of different wood components varies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0020_R_d42357991e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Energy Fuels">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, lignin has the highest thermal stability, while hemicellulose is the lowest, meaning hemicellulose will decompose and pyrolyze first, followed by cellulose, and finally lignin. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0001">1e</a> shows that the untreated Phe experiences significant weight loss near 302 and 393&nbsp;°C during heating. It is well known that the weight loss ≈300 and 600&nbsp;°C can be attributed to the pyrolysis of cellulose.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0021_R_d42357991e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">21</a>, <a href="#smll202407341-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0022_R_d42357991e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to untreated Phe, one weight loss peak appears in H1-Phe earlier accompanied with a shift of the other weight loss peak to a higher temperature, indicating an increase in the mass fraction of unstable components after treatment. Such difference should be attributed to the degradation of some short-segment cellulose and the reduction of cellulose content, resulting in less weight loss in the region of 300–400&nbsp;°C. In addition, the final weight percentage is close to 0%, indicating the content of ash and impurity in wood will not adversely affect the carbonization process, which is conducive to the usage of biomass-derived carbon in the anode of SIBs. Combining FTIR, XRD, HRTEM and TG analysis, it can be concluded that the structure of phellem changes from long-chain cellulose segments to disordered short-segment structure after sulfuric acid treatment, while the chemical composition does not change obviously.</p>
         
         <p>It is worthy to note that sulfuric acid also has the property of oxidation, which may pre-carbonized or pre-oxidize the materials. For understanding the mechanism of such treatment deeply, Phe samples were immersed in sulfuric acid for different periods (0, 0.5, 1, and 2&nbsp;h) followed by washing and drying. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was used to characterize the component and functional groups on the surface of samples. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0001">1f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), the content of O rises first and then stabilizes at ≈20%. Overall, a reaction process dominated by cellulose hydrolysis and mild carbonization oxidation can be established. Initially, sulfuric acid oxidizes the surface of the wood, increasing the number of oxygen-containing functional groups. As the reaction proceeds, the dehydration of the material surface and an increase in the proportion of carbon elements occur. Once the reaction reaches a steady state, the primary reaction is cellulose hydrolysis, which induces structural changes. Moreover, the potential influence of S element should be excluded from the modification and improvement of structure evolution and electrochemical performance development. From the 1s curve of S (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) and the elements evolution over time (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0001">1f</a>), no obvious S peaks can be observed, which means that such treatment did not introduce S heteroatom to the surface of phellem.</p>
         
         <p>Based on the above discussion, a reaction progress dominated by cellulose hydrolysis with mild carbonization and oxidation can be established. However, the connection between the structure evolution in precursors and the microstructure of derived hard carbon is not yet clear. It is very necessary to understand the componential difference of the precursors and structural difference of derived hard carbon after acid treatment and carbonization. The surface differences of the derived hard carbons were further studied. A certain amount of oxygen-containing functional groups and oxygen heteroatoms are detected from the FTIR results (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0021_R_d42357991e722" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">21</a>, <a href="#smll202407341-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0022_R_d42357991e722" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the similar curves indicate that the introduction of acid treatment does not lead to obvious component diversity. From XPS results given in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), similar results of oxygen-containing functional groups can be confirmed. The possibility of extra capacity from S doping is ruled out because there is no obvious S peak (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0023_R_d42357991e734" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Sci.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is clear that the modification of pyrolytic carbon is caused by the change of the internal structure instead of the functional groups or doped atoms on the surface.</p>
         
         <p>The overall structural properties of carbonaceous materials are further provided (<b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#smll202407341-tbl-0001">1</a>). In <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202407341-fig-0002">2a</a>, a left-shift to 23° is observed in H1-Phe-1500 pattern, compared with that of Phe-1500 at ≈26°. For hard carbon, the shift of (0 0 2) peak to the left means that the pseudo-graphitic microcrystalline structure in hard carbon is more disordered with enlarged layer spaces.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0004_R_d42357991e753" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Mater. Today">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to Scherrer formula, the smaller the full width at half maxima (FWHM), the larger the grain size along the c-axis (<i>L</i>c) when the peak position is fixed. The direction of the c-axis of hard carbon is usually defined as perpendicular to the direction of pseudo-graphitic microcrystalline, so a smaller <i>L</i>c means that the thickness of pseudo-graphitic microcrystalline is thinner.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0004_R_d42357991e763" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Mater. Today">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After fitting (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), the (0 0 2) peak FWHM of H1-Phe-1500 is obviously larger than that of Phe-1500, indicating the thinner pseudo-graphitic microcrystalline in H1-Phe-1500. The length information of the carbon layer is obtained by Raman spectroscopy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0002">2b</a>), which generally includes the D band of 1343 cm<sup>−1</sup>, the G band of 1590 cm<sup>−1</sup>, the 2D band of 2680 cm<sup>−1</sup>, and the D+G band near 3&nbsp;000 cm<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0011_R_d42357991e784" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The degree of disorder in hard carbon can be characterized via the relative intensity of D band and G band, as the G band represents the signal of graphene-like layer structure with sp<sup>2</sup> hybrid orbitals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0011_R_d42357991e791" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0002">2b</a>, Raman spectra show that the D band of derived hard carbon increases in the H1-Phe-1500 and the integrated intensity ratio (based on Lorentz fit, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> in Supporting information) of D-band over G-band (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) increases for H1-Phe-1500, revealing the more disordered carbon structure and shorter carbon layers, which are consistent with the decreased thickness of pseudo-graphitic carbon layer for H1-Phe-1500 from the XRD results. Moreover, the intensity of 2D band is significantly weakened, indicating that the graphitization in H1-Phe-1500 is reduced, and the formation of graphite-like microcrystals is inhibited.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0011_R_d42357991e809" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, thinner and shorter pseudo-graphitic microcrystals with enlarged interlayer space can be confirmed in H1-Phe-1500. The acidic treatment mainly acts as hydrolysis with slight oxidation, during which the long segment of cellulose is broken, and transferred to disordered carbon structure in the following carbonization. Clearly, a higher degree of disorder and thinner pseudo-graphitic microcrystals will be helpful to the formation of more effective closed pores for Na storage.</p>

         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202407341-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/da7a2e82-ee48-49b0-82d5-b69bd8ed2de5/smll202407341-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/da7a2e82-ee48-49b0-82d5-b69bd8ed2de5/smll202407341-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/84d520de-efe3-4b13-a058-d807f45e6c8f/smll202407341-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/da7a2e82-ee48-49b0-82d5-b69bd8ed2de5/smll202407341-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202407341-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202407341" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The characterization of pyrolytic carbon: a) XRD patterns; b) Raman spectra; c) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isotherms curves; d) SAXS curves; e) True density test; HRTEM images of Phe-1500 f) and H1-Phe-1500 g).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption measurements based on Brunauer–Emmett–Teller model (BET) were used to detect the porosity characteristics of carbonaceous materials. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0002">2c</a>, the specific surface areas (SSA) of Phe-1500 and H1-Phe-1500 are 45.697 and 4.373 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Compared with Phe-1500, the pore size of H1-Phe-1500 changes significantly, and the total pore volume decreases sharply from 0.095 to 0.013 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, with the pore size mainly distributed in the range of 1–10&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). Note that the SSA and total pore volume of H1-Phe-1500 decrease significantly, which are the symbol of the suppression of open pores in carbon materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0011_R_d42357991e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such a low SSA will reduce the formation of solid−electrolyte interface (SEI) films on the surface of H1-Phe-1500 during the first discharge process, thus improving the ICE. Besides, small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) and true density test, which can effectively identify the closed pore structure, were further carried out. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0002">2d</a> shows the SAXS curves of Phe-1500 and H1-Phe-1500. Comparatively, the latter exhibits a more distinct platform at 1 nm<sup>−1</sup>, which confirms the presence of closed pore structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0004_R_d42357991e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Mater. Today">4</a>, <a href="#smll202407341-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0024_R_d42357991e1078" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Energy Fuels">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Skeletal (true) density analysis using helium (He) as working gas can provide more information of closed pores since helium can enter almost all open pores except closed ones. The true density test for closed pore volume analysis is a powerful and reliable complement to SAXS. Given highly oriented pyrolytic graphite as reference, the total pore volume of carbon materials can be calculated by comparing with the basis (Supporting Information <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0025_R_d42357991e1086" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Power Sources">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The calculation results in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0002">2e</a> prove the formation of abundant closed pores in H1-Phe-1500, leading to a high closed pores volume of 0.235 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>(vs 0.072 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> of Phe-1500). The increased volume of closed pores in H1-Phe-1500 is considered as the most essential difference from the Phe-1500, which is closely related to the different Na&nbsp;storage capability as follows.</p>
         
         <p>HRTEM can demonstrate intuitive analysis for studying the structure of carbonaceous materials. As seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0002">2f,g</a>, both samples have graphite-like structures and turbostratic microstructure, which are typical characteristics of hard carbon, but their microstructures have obvious differences. For Phe-1500 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0002">2f</a>), it has an obvious graphitized structure with more stacking layers (more than 10 layers), in which the carbon layers are orderly arranged with small space (<i>d</i><sub>002</sub>). In contrast, the thickness of the pseudo-graphitic microcrystals in H1-Phe-1500 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0002">2g</a>) is significantly reduced to 3–4 layers, forming some closed pores by random stacking of graphite-like microcrystals. Obviously, from the HRTEM image, we can confirm that the H1-Phe-1500 shows abundant closed pores with thinner pore wall and smaller pore diameter. Moreover, from the selected regions in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0002">2f,g</a>, H1-Phe-1500 exhibits a d<sub>002</sub> interlayer spacing of 0.383&nbsp;nm in average, which is larger than that of 0.374&nbsp;nm for Phe-1500 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). Such results are highly consistent with the analysis of XRD, Raman, and SAXS, further confirming the role of sulfuric acid treatment to crack part of cellulose, inhibit the excessive formation of graphite-like microcrystals during high temperature pyrolysis, and promote the formation of closed pores. According to the reported literature, due to the poor thermodynamic stability of graphite-intercalation compounds (GICs) formed by the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> in graphite-like layers, excessive graphite-like structures are not conducive to Na<sup>+</sup> storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0026_R_d42357991e1132" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ACS Cent. Sci.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the low degree of graphitization and abundant closed pore structure in H1-Phe-1500 could provide more sites and channels for Na<sup>+</sup> migration and storage, which are conductive to the electrochemical performance improvement when used as anode of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>In order to investigate the relationship between the structure and Na storage performance, galvanostatic charge–discharge tests were performed at a current density of 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202407341-fig-0003">3a</a>). Typical charge–discharge curves of hard carbon consist of slope regions and plateau regions, the slope regions corresponds to the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> on the surface and defects, and the plateau area corresponds to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> in the carbon layer and the deposition of Na<sup>+</sup> in the obturator.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0027_R_d42357991e1158" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nano Lett.">27</a>-<a href="#smll202407341-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0030_R_d42357991e1158" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, the reversible specific capacity of H1- Phe-1500 is significantly improved to 330 from 207 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> of Phe-1500. Such difference reveals that the sulfuric acid treatment to the precursors has positive effects on the Na-storage capacity of as-prepared pyrolytic carbon (more closed pores and thinner carbon layers). Compared to Phe-1500, the H1-Phe-1500 holds the advantages in higher ICE (90%&nbsp;vs 78% of Phe-1500) and plateau capacity below 0.1&nbsp;V (230 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> accounting for 70%&nbsp;vs 57% of Phe-1500), which are essential for the energy density of SIBs. From charge–discharge curves and cyclic voltammetry tests (CV) in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), the significantly improved ICE and plateau capacity are probably attributed to the decreased SSA and increased closed pore structure in H1-Phe-1500. The former saves the active Na<sup>+</sup> consumed by the formation of SEI, while the latter enhances the reversibility of Na storage reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0022_R_d42357991e1173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0003">3b</a>, the reversible capacity and ICE achieved in this work are improved comparing with other reported studies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0031_R_d42357991e1181" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Eng. J.">31</a>-<a href="#smll202407341-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0047_R_d42357991e1181" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - RSC Adv.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The rate properties of the two samples are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0003">3c</a>. The reversible capacity of H1-Phe-1500 is higher than that of Phe-1500 at current densities of 20, 50, 100, 200, 500, 1&nbsp;000, and 2&nbsp;000&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. When the current density reaches 2&nbsp;000&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, H1-Phe-1500 still has a capacity of 150 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, followed by the reversible capacity going back to 253 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density returns to 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. Although Phe-1500 has poor rate performance (only ≈100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2&nbsp;000&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), it is worth noting that the gap between the rate capacity of Phe-1500 and H1-Phe-1500 narrows along with the increase of current density. This may be resulted from relatively slow kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> entering closed pores. The long-term cycling performances of the two materials are studied. As seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0003">3d</a>, H1-Phe-1500 exhibits a specific capacity of 290 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles at 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention rate of 80%. Note that the capacity in plateau region was effectively improved via closed pores regulation, which is beneficial to the energy density of full battery. The full batteries were further assembled with O3-Na[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> as cathode (specific capacity 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in a half cell at 0.1C, as displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), which show a high energy density of 247&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on anode and cathode active mass). The specific capacities of full cell are 325, 293, 251, 223, 182 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (based on the mass of anode active materials) at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1 and 2C rates, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0003">3e</a>). When cycled at 1C, the capacity retention rate is 80% after 90 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0003">3f</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202407341-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/48ea822e-77e1-4e0f-96ee-0e9ba5e56038/smll202407341-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/48ea822e-77e1-4e0f-96ee-0e9ba5e56038/smll202407341-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e21c5d21-9564-4cd3-966f-6c3732cb3b5c/smll202407341-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/48ea822e-77e1-4e0f-96ee-0e9ba5e56038/smll202407341-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202407341-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202407341" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical properties of hard carbon and full cells: a) Charge–discharge curves at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>; b) Comparison of reversible capacity and ICE with reported hard carbon materials; c) Rate capability; d) Cycling performance at 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>; e) Rate capability of full-batteries; f) Cycling performance of full cell at 1 C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The dynamic characteristics of the Na storage process in hard carbon are investigated by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), CV test, and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS). The diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>) was calculated by Fick's second law (Supporting Information <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>), and its order is ≈10<sup>−9</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202407341-fig-0004">4b</a>, during the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>, the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> in all samples decrease obviously and reach a minimum value near 0.01&nbsp;V, indicating the slow electrochemical kinetics in the plateau region below 0.1&nbsp;V. For comparison, H1-Phe-1500 shows higher <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> in the slope region, while in the plateau region, the <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> of Phe-1500 is larger. The diffusion kinetics of the H1-Phe-1500 decreases slightly because the Na storage mechanism in the low potential plateau is mainly the deposition of Na<sup>+</sup> into closed pores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0048_R_d42357991e1301" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Adv. Energy Mater.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> CV curves at various scan rates from 0.2 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> are given (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). For the cathodic process, the peak at 0.01&nbsp;V increases with the rising scan rate (ν), which is related to the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in bulk phase and storage in closed pores. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0004">4c</a>, the apparent diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>) can be estimated from the relationship between the peak current (<i>I</i>p) of the cathodic peak and the square root of scan rate (<i>ν</i><sup>1/2</sup>), which is listed in Supporting Information S-6. Based on the slopes in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0004">4c</a>, <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> at ≈0.01&nbsp;V is calculated to be 1.08 × 10<sup>−5</sup> and 6.39 × 10<sup>−6</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> for Phe-1500 and H1-Phe-1500, respectively. The <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> calculated from desodiation process also indicate the slower kinetics in H1-Phe-1500. Generally speaking, H1-Phe-1500 stores Na based on closed-pore-filling at low potential, which makes the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> into the closed pore slower. Based on the above analysis, it can be found that the electrochemical behavior of H1-Phe-1500 in the plateau region is enhanced, further indicating that its high ICE is due to its stronger reversibility in Na storage behavior. This reversibility comes from the abundant closed pore structure and the possible “filling” mechanism inside the pores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0007_R_d42357991e1349" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The used acid treatment effectively promotes the formation of more available closed pore structure by breaking long segmental cellulose into pieces, leading to the obvious performance differences. In terms of electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information), qualitatively, the overall impedance of H1-Phe-1500 is less than that of Phe-1500. Ex-situ EIS was used to investigate the differences in Na storage behaviors. Sampling EIS spectra along the sodiation curves of HC allow the fitting of the distribution of relaxation time (DRT) curves, providing the opportunity to study the kinetics of each individual step of the sodiation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0049_R_d42357991e1357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Electrochim. Acta">49</a>-<a href="#smll202407341-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0051_R_d42357991e1357" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - ChemElectroChem">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Nyquist impedance for each potential versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> shows a small semicircle of ≈5 Ω on the real axis in diameter, followed by another semicircle or open line (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0004">4d,g</a>). The DRT of the cells at each potential are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0004">4e,f,h,i</a>, with each peak corresponding to an RC-element with a characteristic time constant at the center of the peak.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0049_R_d42357991e1371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Electrochim. Acta">49</a>-<a href="#smll202407341-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0051_R_d42357991e1371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - ChemElectroChem">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak at τ = 50 s is interpreted as diffusive mass transfer in electrolyte of Na<sup>+</sup>, while the peaks at 0.1 s and 1 s can be corresponding to the diffusion process in hard carbon behind SEI of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0049_R_d42357991e1381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Electrochim. Acta">49</a>-<a href="#smll202407341-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0051_R_d42357991e1381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - ChemElectroChem">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0004">4f,i</a>, a new electrochemical behavior occurs in H1-Phe-1500 at a potential of 0.01&nbsp;V, resulting in the splitting of the peak located at 1 s into two peaks, while this phenomenon is not observed in Phe-1500. Additionally, as the potential decreases, the peak at τ = 1 s in Phe-1500 shifts toward a larger time constant, whereas in H1-Phe-1500, the peak shifts toward a smaller time constant until it splits into two peaks. These phenomena indicate that the Na storage behavior of H1-Phe-1500 at low potentials is related to the pore filling of Na<sup>+</sup>. This pore filling behavior enhances the reversibility of the electrochemical process and improves the ICE.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202407341-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2d00c967-bc3c-49db-adab-8792e755a4a0/smll202407341-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2d00c967-bc3c-49db-adab-8792e755a4a0/smll202407341-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e64d88da-ef23-498d-99aa-9df990692294/smll202407341-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2d00c967-bc3c-49db-adab-8792e755a4a0/smll202407341-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202407341-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202407341" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium storage behavior analysis of hard carbon. a) GITT curves during the sodiation process; b) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient calculated from GITT; c) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient calculated from CV at different scan rates; d) EISs of Phe-1500 at different potential in half cells; e) the fitted DRT curves of Phe-1500 at different potential in half cells; f) the details of DRT curves from (e); g) EISs of H1-Phe-1500 at different potential in half cells; h) the fitted DRT curves of H1-Phe-1500 at different potential in half cells; i) the details of DRT curves from (h).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Finally, for practical applications, we systematically studied the universality of this strategy, especially under different carbonization temperatures and other types of low-value biomass. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202407341-fig-0005">5a</a> summarizes and compares the Raman spectra of phellem-derived hard carbon samples carbonized at various temperatures. As the pyrolysis temperature increases, the relative intensity of the G peak gradually increases, indicating that higher temperature is conducive to the formation of graphite-like layer structure, because temperature is an important driving force for rearrangement of carbon atoms during the pyrolysis process. Via acid treatment, it can be found that <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> increases obviously and the relative intensity of the 2D peak which symbolizes the graphite-like structure, decreases significantly, indicating that sulfuric acid cracking cellulose has the effect of inhibiting rearrangement to form graphitic-like structure for each temperature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0052_R_d42357991e1431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is conducive to the formation of disordered structures and closed pores, thus increasing the Na storage capacity. Taking pyrolytic carbon at 1100&nbsp;°C as an instance, from the HRTEM images and SAXS curves comparison in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0005">5b</a>, the formation of pseudo-graphitic microcrystals is inhibited effectively, which is replaced by the disordered stack of carbon layers and closed pores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0053_R_d42357991e1439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Small">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The analysis of XRD in Supporting Information Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> (Supporting Information) can also prove the universality of this strategy. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0005">5c</a> exhibits the total capacity and plateau capacity of various carbons, which are summarized from Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information). The part marked with light pink is the plateau capacity difference between the treated and untreated samples, which is approximately equal to the difference in total capacity. The capacity improvement is mainly contributed by the plateau capacity, indicating that this sulfuric acid hydrolysis strategy can effectively promote the Na storage process in low voltage, which is benefit to developing higher energy density SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202407341-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202407341-bib-0028_R_d42357991e1454" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Energy Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Peanut shells and rosewood were also treated in the same way for the universality study. In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0005">5d</a>, the carbonized peanut shell without treatment (PeaS-1500) has an obvious graphitization-like structure. In contrast, in H1-PeaS-1500, some closed pores via random stacking of graphite-like crystallites are formed. This conclusion is consistent with XRD and Raman analysis in (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16–S18</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that sulfuric acid treatment can still have positive effects on peanut shells. As for Na storage performance, H1-PeaS-1500 has a higher reversible capacity of 300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, higher than that of the untreated sample (≈260 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>), and the improvement in capacity mainly occurs in the platform region (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0005">5e</a>). The PeaS-1500 and H1-PeaS-1500 have relatively stable cyclic properties at 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information). In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202407341-fig-0005">5f</a>, the platform capacity of H1-RW-1500 increases from 210 to 250 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which also proves the effect of acid treatment on rosewood. All these results strongly demonstrate that the strategy of sulfuric acid hydrolysis of biomass precursors is universal for other carbonization temperatures and species.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202407341-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8e39ddb0-2eb3-4cd6-90d9-5bfeb8cd7d9b/smll202407341-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8e39ddb0-2eb3-4cd6-90d9-5bfeb8cd7d9b/smll202407341-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e16f0f3e-ed43-4c05-bfc4-ee5475c65eef/smll202407341-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8e39ddb0-2eb3-4cd6-90d9-5bfeb8cd7d9b/smll202407341-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202407341-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202407341" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Raman spectra of carbons pyrolyzed at different temperatures; b) SAXS curves of Phe-1100 and H1-Phe-1100 (inside: HRTEM images of Phe-1100 and H1-Phe-1100); c) Summarized performance of carbons pyrolyzed at different temperatures; d) HRTEM images of untreated peanut shell pyrolytic carbon (upper) and hydrolyzed peanut shell derived carbon (below); e) charge–discharge curves peanut shells derived hard carbons; f) charge–discharge curves of rosewood derived hard carbons. Current density: 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202407341-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202407341-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a universal regulating strategy for closed pore structure in waste biomass-derived hard carbon was proposed, which could enable the activation of low-cost and low-value biomass as a promising precursor for hard carbon preparation. Using waste thermos bottle stoppers as an example, cellulose hydrolysis by sulphuric acid would partly break its long chain structure resulting in the formation of more effective closed pores during the carbonization, thus providing sufficient sites for Na<sup>+</sup> deposition or filling. The as-prepared H1-Phe-1500 showed significantly improved Na storage performance with a higher reversible capacity of 330 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (70% platform capacity) and a higher ICE of 90%. While the capacity and ICE of the untreated sample are 207 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 78%, respectively. Benefiting from high ICE and low operating potential, the assembled full cell showed an energy density of 247&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. This universal strategy can be applied to other hard carbon manufacturing conditions and waste biomass carbon sources, providing ideas for future biomass-derived carbon design and modification.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202407341-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202407341-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202407341-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202407341-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202407341-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202407341-sec-0070-title"> Materials Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>First, the phellem plug (waste thermos bottle stopper) was soaked in deionized water to remove impurities, dried, and then ground into 0.1–1&nbsp;mm diameter powder. Sulfuric acid solution with the concentration of 64 wt.% was prepared, and an appropriate amount of phellem plug powder was added and heated at 45&nbsp;°C for 1 h. Then dispersed in a large amount of deionized water to terminate the reaction. After the sample settled, the supernatant was removed and replaced with deionized water. This process was repeated until the supernatant became neutral. The samples were then dried at 80&nbsp;°C and labeled as “H1-Phe” (treated) and “Phe” (untreated). The dried samples were calcined in a corundum crucible within a tubular furnace under an Ar atmosphere. The temperature was raised from room temperature to a specific temperature (900&nbsp;, 1100&nbsp;, 1300&nbsp;, 1500&nbsp;°C) at a rate of 2&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> and maintained for 3 h. After calcination, the samples were cooled to room temperature and labeled as “Phe-x” and “H1-Phe-x”, respectively. For universality study among different species, peanut shells and rosewood were treated in the same way, and the treated precursors and pyrolytic carbon materials were obtained. Untreated peanut precursors were denoted as PeaS, treated peanut precursors were denoted as H1-PeaS, and pyrolytic carbon under 1500&nbsp;°C were denoted as PeaS-1500 and H1-PeaS-1500, respectively. Rosewood was treated via the same way.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202407341-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202407341-sec-0080-title"> Materials Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>In this paper, SEM was used to obtain the surface morphological characteristics of the materials. The SEM was performed on a Quanta 400 FEG (FEI Company) instrument with an accelerated voltage of 5&nbsp;kV. Crystal structures of carbon materials were observed using HRTEM, carried out on Titan ETEM G2 80–300 (FEI Company). The lattice of carbon materials was analyzed by XRD in A PANalytical Empyrean 2 instrument with a copper target (λ = 1.54 Å) as the light source. Raman spectra of carbon materials were obtained with the help of LabRAM JY-HR 800 spectrometer with a wavelength of 532&nbsp;nm, and the test range was 500–4&nbsp;000 cm<sup>−1</sup>. SAXS was performed with Xenocs Nano-InXider with a Cu Kα light source to study the obturator structure of carbon materials. True density tests were performed on the AccuPyc II 1340 with helium as the working gas. FTIR and XPS were used to study the surface composition of carbon materials. FTIR was performed by a Thermos Nicolet iS50 FTIR spectrophotometer in transmission mode using potassium bromide particles. XPS was tested with Thermo Kalpha under a monochromatic AlKa source (Mono AlKa) with an energy of 1486.6&nbsp;eV. The thermal stability and composition of the wood-based biomass were analyzed by a TG at a temperature range of 50–600&nbsp;°C and a warming rate of 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> with atmosphere of air. Specific surface area and pore size were analyzed using JW-BK200 (JWGB SCI.&amp; TECH).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202407341-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202407341-sec-0090-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>CR2016 (half-cell) and CR2025 (full-cell) coin-type cells were used for electrochemical testing of the carbon materials. Carbon anodes were prepared by mixing active materials, Super P, and sodium alginate in a 90:5:5 mass ratio with deionized water, then casting the slurry onto Cu foil and drying at 80&nbsp;°C under vacuum for 8 h. The mass loading of the active material was ≈2–3&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. Pure Na foil was used as the counter electrode, 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">M</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in dimethyl ether (DME) as the electrolyte, and glass fiber as the separator. Half-cell assembly was performed in an argon-filled glove box (MIKROUNA) with water and oxygen concentrations below 1&nbsp;ppm. Charge–discharge testing and GITT were conducted on the Neware BTS 7.6 Battery Test System. Charge and discharge cycles were carried out at different current densities with cut-off voltage of 0.01-2.00&nbsp;V. GITT parameters were set to a 0.5 h current pulse duration at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> and a 2 h stagnation time. CV and EIS tests were performed using a CHI660e Electrochemical Workstation. Sodium-ion full cells were assembled with H1-Phe-1500 as anode materials and Na[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> (Xiangying Co. LTD, Jiangsu, China) as cathode materials, with a mass ratio of 1:2.75–3 (anode: cathode, cathode mass was exceeded). NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) was used as the electrolyte. Full cells were charged and discharged with cut-off voltage of 2 and 4.2&nbsp;V.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
